<div class="m-a-box-content-bottom"></div><!-- End of .m-a-box-content-bottom -->        </div><!-- End of .m-a-box-profile -->

        
    </div><!-- End of .m-a-box-container -->

	
</div><!-- End of .m-a-box -->
<p>Malware is constantly evolving to become more evasive, destructive, efficient, and infectious. There are numerous families of malware, each with its own unique characteristics. These different families of malware can work together in a symphonious manner to deliver a powerful infection. For instance, the stealer malware can exfiltrate data before the ransomware encrypts the files.&nbsp;</p>



<p>In this blog post, we&#8217;re diving into a recent case of something I started calling a &#8220;malware symphony.&#8221; It&#8217;s a way to describe how different types of malware can work together, sort of like instruments in an orchestra. And just like how each instrument adds to the harmony, these malware parts work together in a coordinated way — we&#8217;ll explore the behavior of each malware involved in this symphony in detail.</p>



<p>Let’s dive right into it!</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Overview of CrackedCantil</h2>



<p>The author (of this article, not the malware), <a href="https://lambdamamba.com/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">Lena (aka LambdaMamba)</a> has decided to name this type of malware the “<strong>CrackedCantil</strong>”.&nbsp;</p>



<p>The “Cracked” part comes from cracked software, which is a common vector of infection. The “Cantil” part comes from the Cantil Viper, which is a species of highly venomous viper. This viper uses its bright yellow tail to lure in prey, just like how this malware uses cracked software to lure in victims.&nbsp;</p>



<p>And just like viper venom, which uses a complex cocktail of chemicals that work together to wreak havoc in the victim’s body, numerous malware work together in the CrackedCantil to wreak havoc in the victim’s system. The CrackedCantil examined in this article includes the following:</p>



<ul>
<li>Loaders: Includes the <a href="https://any.run/cybersecurity-blog/privateloader-analyzing-the-encryption-and-decryption-of-a-modern-loader/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">PrivateLoader</a> and <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/smoke" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Smoke</a>, which drops more malware onto the system</li>



<li>Infostealers: Includes the <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/lumma" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Lumma</a>, <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/redline" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">RedLine</a>, <a href="https://any.run/cybersecurity-blog/risepro-malware-communication-analysis/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">RisePro</a>, Amadey, Stealc, which steals sensitive information </li>



<li>Crytominers: Turns the infected system into a cryptominer, which drains system resources&nbsp;</li>



<li>Proxy Bot Malware: Includes the Socks5Systemz, which turns the infected system into a proxy bot</li>



<li>Ransomware: Includes the STOP, which encrypts the files and disrupts the system</li>
</ul>



<p>Also, the process tree is long, packed, and intertwined like a snake pit.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" width="1024" height="465" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-1024x465.png" alt="The CrackedCantil Process Tree and an image of a Snake Pit generated by OpenAI" class="wp-image-6780" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-1024x465.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-300x136.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-768x349.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-1536x698.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-370x168.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-270x123.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min-740x336.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image26-min.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The CrackedCantil Process Tree and an image of a Snake Pit generated by OpenAI</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Analyzing the behavior in a sandbox</h2>



<p>The CrackedCantil ANY.RUN sample examined in this article can be found <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/7c196a3f-2132-4855-ac98-176fa600c299/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">here</a>. Additionally, the analysis techniques introduced in my blog <a href="https://any.run/cybersecurity-blog/analyzing-snake-keylogger/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Analyzing Snake Keylogger in ANY.RUN: a Full Walkthrough</a> will be used here.&nbsp;</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Searching for the Cracked software&nbsp;</h3>



<p>The query “ida pro crack download” was searched on Google on a Windows 11 Google Chrome using a United States Residential Proxy. There was a peculiar Google Groups result “CRACK IDA Pro V6 8 150423 And HEX-Rays Decompiler …” within the first search result page:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-1024x575.png" alt="Searching “ida pro crack download” on Google" class="wp-image-6781" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-1536x863.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87-740x416.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image87.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Searching “ida pro crack download” on Google</figcaption></figure>

<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-1024x575.png" alt="A Google Groups conversation with a download link" class="wp-image-6782" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-1536x862.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image64.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">A Google Groups conversation with a download link</figcaption></figure>

<p>The archive file is password-protected and can be opened with the password provided on the download site, which was “1234”. A folder called “prom” and an application called “setup.exe” are inside the archive. These were extracted onto the Desktop:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-1024x575.png" alt="The contents of “release.rar”" class="wp-image-6784" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-1536x863.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23-740x416.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image23.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The contents of “release.rar”</figcaption></figure>

<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Running the Cracked Software</h3>



<p>Double-clicking on “setup.exe” will execute the application. Around a minute after executing “setup.exe”, a bunch of processes is spawned, and “cmd.exe” and “schtasks.exe” windows pop up:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="576" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-1024x576.png" alt="The “cmd.exe” and “schtasks.exe” windows pop up after executing “setup.exe”" class="wp-image-6786" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-1024x576.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-768x432.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-1536x864.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68-740x416.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image68.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “cmd.exe” and “schtasks.exe” windows pop up after executing “setup.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<p>After “cmd.exe” and “schtasks.exe” closes, nothing alarming happens from the user’s perspective. The system is manually restarted for experimentation. The system restarts normally from the user’s perspective, and logins to “admin”.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large is-resized"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-1024x574.png" alt="Logging into “admin” after manual system reboot" class="wp-image-6787" width="650" height="364" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-1024x574.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-768x430.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-1536x861.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-370x207.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-270x151.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image89.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 650px) 100vw, 650px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Logging into “admin” after manual system reboot</figcaption></figure>



<p>After the login, everything on the Desktop looks normal from the user’s perspective. Approximately 15 seconds later, a “schtasks.exe” window pops up and a bunch of processes are spawned:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-1024x575.png" alt="The “schtasks.exe” window pops up after logging in" class="wp-image-6788" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-1536x862.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image72.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “schtasks.exe” window pops up after logging in</figcaption></figure>



<p>A few seconds later, the files’ icons change to a white file icon, and the “.hhaz” extension is added to the files, indicating they were encrypted:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="576" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-1024x576.png" alt="The files’ type has become “HHAZ file”" class="wp-image-6789" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-1024x576.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-768x432.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-1536x864.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10-740x416.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image10.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The files’ type has become “HHAZ file”</figcaption></figure>



<p>The ransom note is located in “C:\Users\admin\_readme.txt”. The ransom note includes a link to download the decryption instruction video, the contact email, and a personal ID:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-1024x575.png" alt="Contents of the ransom note “_readme.txt”" class="wp-image-6790" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-1536x862.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image85.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Contents of the ransom note “_readme.txt”</figcaption></figure>



<p>In a <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/f344d645-ff6f-4453-a115-4f721a3ea7c5/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">different ANY.RUN task</a>, the WeTransfer link was opened in a browser, and the”Decrypt Software.avi” was downloaded and opened in a Video player. It showed a decryption instruction video with a “.djvuu” example:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="574" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-1024x574.png" alt="The Decryption Instruction video from the ransom note" class="wp-image-6791" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-1024x574.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-1536x861.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-370x207.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-270x151.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image21.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The Decryption Instruction video from the ransom note</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Analyzing the Processes based on Malware Family</h2>



<p>The process tree is complex, and numerous notorious malware families were involved. This section will break down the different malware families involved and explore each one in detail.&nbsp; They include PrivateLoader, Smoke, Lumma, RedLine, RisePro, Amadey, Stealc, Socks5Systemz, and STOP.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">PrivateLoader</h2>



<p>PrivateLoader is a malicious loader family first identified in 2021 and is known for distributing many kinds of malware including stealers, rootkits, spyware, and more. It is written in C++, and cracked software is a common source of infection. Additionally, it drops payloads depending on the configuration of the victim’s system. More information on PrivateLoader can be found in <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/privateloader" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s PrivateLoader Malware Trends</a>.&nbsp;</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 4440: setup.exe</h3>



<p>The process “setup.exe” (process 1952) starts when the “setup.exe” executable is double-clicked from the Desktop. Almost immediately after, another process called “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns, and is detected as a PrivateLoader. From Process 4440, numerous malicious processes spawn, which includes more PrivateLoader instances, Smoke, Lumma, RedLine, RisePro, Amadey, Stealc, Socks5Systemz, and STOP.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="584" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-1024x584.png" alt="The “setup.exe” (process 4440) is detected as a PrivateLoader" class="wp-image-6793" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-1024x584.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-768x438.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-1536x877.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4-740x422.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image4.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “setup.exe” (process 4440) is detected as a PrivateLoader</figcaption></figure>



<p>Numerous executables are downloaded by “setup.exe” (process 4440) from several endpoints. Detonating these executables independently inside the ANY.RUN Sandbox revealed that they are Stealc (<a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/aa525e4f-df59-4fa9-8c30-ff9319146363?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">timeSync.exe ANY.RUN task</a>), Redline (<a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/69c079e0-0a60-45c4-b892-25f8f1783ce6?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">autorun.exe ANY.RUN task</a>), Risepro (<a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/380d81ab-8731-4e02-a5b1-11b98ee89ee8?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">good.exe ANY.RUN task</a>), and Sock5Systemz (<a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/769e69e6-cd08-43e4-aa76-53149876a4fe?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">adobe.exe ANY.RUN task</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="136" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-1024x136.png" alt="Process 4440 downloads several executables from several endpoints" class="wp-image-6794" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-1024x136.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-300x40.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-768x102.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-1536x204.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-370x49.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-270x36.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42-740x99.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image42.png 1908w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 4440 downloads several executables from several endpoints</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4440 is also seen communicating with its C2 server, 185[.]216.70.235 and 195.20.16[.]45 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>). HTTP requests <em>“/api/tracemap.php”</em> and <em>“/api/firegate.php” </em>were made to the host 185[.]216.70.235 and 195.20.16[.]45 by Process 4440:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="122" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-1024x122.png" alt="The HTTP requests by Process 4440" class="wp-image-6795" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-1024x122.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-300x36.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-768x92.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-1536x183.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-370x44.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-270x32.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5-740x88.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image5.png 1694w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The HTTP requests by Process 4440</figcaption></figure>



<p>An example network stream between 195.20.16[.]45:80 and VM:52634 can be seen below:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="418" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-1024x418.png" alt="The Network Stream for Process 4440 between 195.20.16[.]45:80 and VM:52634" class="wp-image-6796" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-1024x418.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-300x122.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-768x314.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-1536x627.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-370x151.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-270x110.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67-740x302.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image67.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 4440<em> </em>between 195.20.16[.]45:80 and VM:52634</figcaption></figure>



<p>The contents include Base64-encoded strings, like “<em>Q0uWGgHyOK1yWQK-BXHkM-HySJVrM-bkDRjaZRMVle11OCvYaPf2WzR9nGuLpCPzAv8ibLyhynT0DqT5CPejzN_j4vkuL4Rmafqdqg7q29RNzn9VOTArbMt6Jrq5lsZ3</em>” , but decoding these strings did not reveal human-readable results. These strings are encrypted, and decrypting may reveal the C2 server and other crucial information as shown in <a href="https://any.run/cybersecurity-blog/privateloader-analyzing-the-encryption-and-decryption-of-a-modern-loader/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">PrivateLoader: Analyzing the Encryption and Decryption of a Modern Loader.</a></p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 5088: vRNddZqIkwaYVpHLFkGcr1Tk.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “vRNddZqIkwaYVpHLFkGcr1Tk.exe” (process 5088), which is also detected as&nbsp; PrivateLoader.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="583" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-1024x583.png" alt="The “setup.exe” (process 4440) &gt; “vRNddZqIkwaYVpHLFkGcr1Tk.exe” (process 5088) is detected as a PrivateLoader." class="wp-image-6797" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-1024x583.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-768x437.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-1536x874.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3-740x421.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image3.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “setup.exe” (process 4440) </figcaption></figure>



<p>The “vRNddZqIkwaYVpHLFkGcr1Tk.exe” (process 5088) was seen modifying files in the Chrome extension folder. Browser extensions can be abused to establish persistent access to systems (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1176/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1176 &#8211; Browser Extensions</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="349" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-1024x349.png" alt="Process 5088 is seen modifying files in the Chrome extension folder" class="wp-image-6798" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-1024x349.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-300x102.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-768x262.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-370x126.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-270x92.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19-740x252.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image19.png 1422w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 5088 is seen modifying files in the Chrome extension folder</figcaption></figure>



<p>The extension “difpelfbkngealhghppkgcpkgbgohhph” is associated with <a href="https://chromewebstore.google.com/detail/k-searches/difpelfbkngealhghppkgcpkgbgohhph?pli=1" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">K Searches</a>. According to the K Searches description, “The extension will update your search settings and will change your new tab search provider to Microsoft Bing&#8221;. Opening Google Chrome on a different ANY.RUN task after detonating “setup.exe” showed the K Searches extension being added to the browser:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="575" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-1024x575.png" alt="The K Searches extension on a Chrome Browser" class="wp-image-6799" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-1024x575.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-768x431.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-1536x862.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43-740x415.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image43.png 1915w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The K Searches extension on a Chrome Browser</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 5088 also communicates with its C2, 195.20.16[.]45 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>), and the HTTP POST requests also contain Base64 encoded and encrypted strings just like Process 4440:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="442" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-1024x442.png" alt="The Network Stream " class="wp-image-6801" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-1024x442.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-300x129.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-768x331.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-1536x663.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2048x883.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-370x160.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-270x116.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-740x319.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 5088<em> </em>between 195.20.16[.]45:80 and VM:53329</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 1916: wlC578T8hWfvZ2yJxLzrF38Y.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “wlC578T8hWfvZ2yJxLzrF38Y.exe” (process 1916), which is also detected as&nbsp; PrivateLoader.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="530" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-1024x530.png" alt="" class="wp-image-6802" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-1024x530.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-300x155.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-768x398.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-1536x795.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-370x192.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-270x140.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37-740x383.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image37.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “setup.exe” (process 4440) </figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 1916 was seen dropping executables “C:\Users\admin\Pictures\Minor Policy\5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe[.]exe” and “C:\Users\admin\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Windows\INetCache\IE\J0KBFYBW\build2[1].exe”. These two executables have the same hash, and “5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe[.]exe” is examined in a later section, which is detected as Amadey.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="286" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-1024x286.png" alt="Process 1916 drops executable files immediately after the start" class="wp-image-6803" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-1024x286.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-300x84.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-768x215.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-1536x430.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-370x103.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-270x76.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70-740x207.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image70.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 1916 drops executable files immediately after the start</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 5088 also communicates with its C2, 45.15.156[.]229 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>). Similar to Process 4440 and Process 5088, the HTTP POST requests also contain Base64 encoded and encrypted strings:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="436" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-1024x436.png" alt="The Network Stream for Process 1916 between 45.15.156[.]229:80 and VM:52754" class="wp-image-6804" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-1024x436.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-300x128.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-768x327.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-1536x654.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-2048x871.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-370x157.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-270x115.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-2-740x315.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 1916<em> </em>between 45.15.156[.]229:80 and VM:52754</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Smoke&nbsp;</h2>



<p>Smoke is a modular malware first identified in 2011, and is known to download other malware as well as steal information. The Smoke Loader can load several files, execute them, mimic legitimate processes, and more. It injects malicious code into system processes like “explorer.exe”, and conducts malicious activities while evading detection. More information on the Smoke Loader can be found in <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/smoke" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s Smoke Loader Malware Trends</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 4192: explorer.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “vvlbVE_a1T9mi81qLqDvAjYH.exe” (process 2648), which runs injected code in “explorer.exe” (process 4192). This is detected as Smoke.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="579" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-1024x579.png" alt="Smoke malware" class="wp-image-6805" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-1024x579.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-300x170.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-768x435.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-1536x869.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-370x209.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-270x153.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46-740x419.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image46.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “setup.exe” (process 4440) </figcaption></figure>



<p>The &#8220;C:\Users\admin\Pictures\Minor Policy\vvlbVE_a1T9mi81qLqDvAjYH.exe&#8221; is responsible for injecting malicious code into “explorer.exe”:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="952" height="446" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6806" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33.png 952w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33-300x141.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33-768x360.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33-370x173.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33-270x126.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image33-740x347.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 952px) 100vw, 952px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “vvlbVE_a1T9mi81qLqDvAjYH.exe” (process 2648) runs injected code in another process</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “explorer.exe” (process 4192) conducts several malicious activities after being injected with malicious code. Process 4192 is seen communicating with the C2 servers, 34.94.245[.]237, 91.215.85[.]17, 34.168.225[.]46 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>).</p>



<p>HTTP POST requests “<em>/”</em> to the host<em> sumagulituyo[.]org, stualialuyastrelia[.]net, criogetikfenbut[.]org </em>were observed for 34.94.245[.]237, 91.215.85[.]17, 34.168.225[.]46 respectively for Process 4192. The response to the POST requests contained references to <em>https://myattwg.att[.]com/UverseAccount.html, </em>and opening this URL in a browser in <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/85df2a11-3e26-454e-b7b7-3455c79c0f1a/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN sandbox</a> shows a site that asks for AT&amp;T credentials. This is known to be a browser hijacker according to OSINT.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="291" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-1024x291.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6807" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-1024x291.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-300x85.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-768x218.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-1536x436.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-370x105.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-270x77.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61-740x210.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image61.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The Network<em> </em>Stream<em> </em>comparison </figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4192 runs a command that uses PowerShell to tell the Windows Defender to ignore the current user’s profile folder ( “C:\Users\admin” in this case), and the Program Files folder (“C:\Program Files” in this case) during scans. This allows more malware into the system without being detected by Windows Defender (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1562/001/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1562.001 &#8211; Impair Defenses: Disable or Modify Tools</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="513" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-1024x513.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6809" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-1024x513.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-300x150.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-768x384.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-1536x769.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-370x185.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-270x135.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24-740x370.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image24.png 1578w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 4192 adds a path to the Windows Defender exclusion list with the line surrounded by green</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4192 runs a command that will start a scheduled task called “<em>GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC” </em>using <em>schtasks </em>(Task Scheduler). The purpose is to evade analysis environments with time-based methods, and the Windows Task Scheduler can be abused for the initial or recurring execution of malicious code (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1497/003/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1497.003 &#8211; Virtualization/Sandbox Evasion: Time Based Evasion</a>, and <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1053/005/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1053.005 &#8211; Scheduled Task/Job: Scheduled Task</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="515" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-1024x515.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6810" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-1024x515.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-300x151.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-768x386.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-1536x772.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-370x186.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-270x136.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1-740x372.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image8-1.png 1580w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 4192 uses the Task Scheduler to run other applications with the line surrounded by green</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC” is an XML file that is modified by “svchost.exe” (process 1272). The “svchost.exe” is located under “C:\Windows\system32\”. This is a system file in Windows, and acts as a host process for services running from DLLs.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="74" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-1024x74.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6811" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-1024x74.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-300x22.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-768x56.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-1536x111.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-370x27.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-270x20.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81-740x54.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image81.png 1852w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 1272 modifies the file “GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC”</figcaption></figure>



<p>“GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC” is an XML configuration file for a scheduled task. It configures a scheduled task called “GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC”, which will be triggered at every system boot. It runs using the highest available privilege, and will execute “C:\Program Files\Google\Chrome\updater.exe”.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="222" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-1024x222.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6812" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-1024x222.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-300x65.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-768x167.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-1536x333.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-370x80.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-270x59.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12-740x161.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image12.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The URI is “GoogleUpdateTaskMachineQC”</figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="156" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-1024x156.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6813" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-1024x156.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-300x46.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-768x117.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-1536x234.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-370x56.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-270x41.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58-740x113.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image58.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The RunLevel is “HighestAvailable”</figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="131" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-1024x131.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6814" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-1024x131.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-300x38.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-768x98.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-1536x197.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-370x47.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-270x35.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56-740x95.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image56.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The Exec location is “C:\Program Files\Google\Chrome\updater.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “t4vXjCz8dD8LVP0hkcsFvzr1.exe” (process 6320) spawns from the PrivateLoader “wlC578T8hWfvZ2yJxLzrF38Y.exe” (process 1916), and modifies “C:\Program Files\Google\Chrome\updater.exe”:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="77" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-1024x77.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6815" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-1024x77.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-300x23.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-768x58.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-1536x116.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-370x28.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-270x20.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15-740x56.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image15.png 1854w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 6320 modifies “C:\Program Files\Google\Chrome\updater.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<p>Detonating “updater.exe” <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/ba5fb117-170b-4df2-94bc-b8b1d5728984?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">in this sample,</a> independently in ANY.RUN sandbox revealed that it is a Miner malware.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="399" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-1024x399.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6816" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-1024x399.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-300x117.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-768x299.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-1536x599.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-2048x798.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-370x144.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-270x105.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-3-740x289.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The attributes of “updater.exe” in <em>Static Discovering</em></figcaption></figure>



<p>After the system reboot, “updater.exe” (process 1632) starts via Task Scheduler:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="583" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-1024x583.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6817" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-1024x583.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-768x438.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-1536x875.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13-740x422.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image13.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">System Reboot &gt; “updater.exe” (process 1632)</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 1632 drops executable files “C:\Program Files\Google\Libs\WR64.sys”, and “C:\Windows\TEMP\cwpxsctaqxko.tmp”.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="284" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-1024x284.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6818" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-1024x284.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-300x83.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-768x213.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-1536x426.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-370x103.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-270x75.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1-740x205.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image1.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 1632 drops executable files immediately after reboot</figcaption></figure>



<p>In the “WR64.sys” and “cwpxsctaqxko.tmp” EXIF information, the MachineType mentioned “AMD AMD64”. According to OSINT, these files are Miner malware for AMD64.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="405" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-1024x405.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6819" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-1024x405.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-300x119.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-768x304.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-1536x607.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-2048x810.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-370x146.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-270x107.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/screenshot-4-740x293.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The attributes of “WR64.sys” and “cwpxsctaqxko.tmp” in <em>Static Discovering</em></figcaption></figure>

<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 1436: explorer.exe</h3>



<p>After the system reboot, “bdutbcd” (process 3984) injects “explorer.exe” (process 1436), and this is detected as Smoke.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large is-resized"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-1024x583.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6821" width="650" height="370" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-1024x583.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-768x437.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-1536x874.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91-740x421.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image91.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 650px) 100vw, 650px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">System reboot &gt; “bdutbcd” (process 3984) ↦ “explorer.exe” (process 1436) is detected as Smoke</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 3984 originates from the initial Smoke instance before reboot, “explorer.exe” (process 4192). “bdutbcd” has the exact same hash as “vvlbVE_a1T9mi81qLqDvAjYH.exe”, which injected “explorer.exe” (process 4192):</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="94" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-1024x94.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6822" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-1024x94.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-300x27.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-768x70.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-1536x140.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-370x34.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-270x25.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40-740x68.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image40.png 1686w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">“C:\Users\admin\AppData\Roaming\bdutbcd” originates from Process 4192</figcaption></figure>



<p>Numerous HTTP POST requests to several hosts and IPs were observed for Process 1436:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="511" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-1024x511.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6823" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-1024x511.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-300x150.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-768x383.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-1536x767.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-370x185.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-270x135.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53-740x369.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image53.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The HTTP POST requests and the <em>Network Stream</em> for Process 1436</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Lumma</h2>



<p>Lumma is an information stealer first identified in 2022. It is developed using the C programming language and is known to steal sensitive information such as cryptocurrency wallets, credentials, and more. Lumma can target a wide range of systems, ranging from Windows 7 up to 11, and has been actively evolving since its discovery. More information on Lumma can be found in <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/lumma" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s Lumma Malware Trends.</a></p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 1588: T6OBqC4lLuNgq7EqPk6LjxrX.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “T6OBqC4lLuNgq7EqPk6LjxrX.exe” (process 2344), which also spawns “T6OBqC4lLuNgq7EqPk6LjxrX.exe” (process 1588). This is detected as Lumma.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="585" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-1024x585.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6824" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-1024x585.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-768x439.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-1536x877.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48-740x423.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image48.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The “setup.exe” (process 4440)</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 1588 was also seen connecting to its C2 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>), and HTTP POST requests “<em>/api” </em>to the host <em>cinemaretailermkw[.]fun </em>were observed.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="301" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1024x301.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6826" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1024x301.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-300x88.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-768x226.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1536x452.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-370x109.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-270x79.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-740x218.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55.png 1902w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">&nbsp;The HTTP POST requests made by Process 1588</figcaption></figure>



<p>In one of the POST requests to the host <em>cinemaretailermkw[.]fun</em>, the string “<em>Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&#8221;file&#8221;; filename=&#8221;file&#8221;</em>” and “<em>Content-Type: attachment/x-object</em>” were observed (in green). This indicates that the content underneath is a file. Strings like “<em>System.txt</em>”, “<em>Software.txt</em>”, and “<em>Screen.png</em>” (in red) were observed within the content, which suggests that this file is an archive file.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="411" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-1024x411.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6827" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-1024x411.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-300x121.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-768x309.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-1536x617.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-370x149.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-270x108.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36-740x297.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image36.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 1588<em> </em>between 188.114.97[.]3:80 and VM:56670</figcaption></figure>



<p>The PCAP was downloaded, and the file contents were extracted from “<em>MIME Multipart Media Encapsulation, Type: multipart/form-data, Boundary: &#8220;be85de5ipdocierre1</em>&#8221; &gt; “<em>Media Type</em>” &gt; “<em>Export Packet Bytes…</em>” with Wireshark. The file was named “file.zip”.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="481" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-1024x481.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6828" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-1024x481.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-300x141.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-768x361.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-1536x722.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-370x174.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-270x127.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min-740x348.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image57-min.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Extracting “file.zip” from the PCAP using Wireshark</figcaption></figure>



<p>This “file.zip” was opened inside a <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/74a3e04a-322d-43c2-b6dd-5270d5094853?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">new ANY.RUN sandbox&#8217;s sample</a>. This archive file contains “System.txt”, “Software.txt”, and “Screen.png”. Opening “Screen.png” shows a screenshot of the original <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/7c196a3f-2132-4855-ac98-176fa600c299/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">CrackedCantil task</a> at 6:31 AM:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="286" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-1024x286.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6829" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-1024x286.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-300x84.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-768x214.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-1536x429.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-370x103.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-270x75.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14-740x207.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image14.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Opening “file.zip” with WinRAR, and “Screen.png in Photos</figcaption></figure>



<p>Opening “Software.txt” and “System.txt” in Notepad showed a bunch of interesting information. “Software.txt” contained the information of installed software in the ANY.RUN sandbox system. “System.txt&#8221; contained the Lumma ID, the Telegram (@lummanowork), and system information like the PC name, user, OS Version, HWID, Screen Resolution, Language, CPU Name, GPU, Physical Installed Memory.</p>

<p>In another POST request to the host <em>cinemaretailermkw[.]fun</em>, something similar was observed. Strings like “<em>Edge/BrowserVersion.txt</em>”, “<em>Edge/dp.txt</em>”, and “<em>Edge/Default/History</em>” (in red) were observed within the content, which suggests that this file is also an archive file.</p>

<p>The PCAP was downloaded, and the file contents were extracted with the method highlighted previously. <figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="411" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-1024x411.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6835" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-1024x411.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-300x121.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-768x309.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-1536x617.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-2048x823.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-370x149.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-270x108.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/8-740x297.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 1588<em> </em>between 188.114.97[.]3:80 and VM:54018</figcaption></figure>



<p>The file contents were extracted, and named “file3.zip”. It was opened inside an <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/c55e5b17-9428-4c51-81f8-74f95dc513dd?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN sample</a>, which contained a folder “Mozilla Firefox”:</p>
