<p>The information in the archive files are exfiltrated via HTTP by Process 1588.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 4360: RegSvcs.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “cuS4AGoWkhss2UsAPWfpvGrK.exe” (process 2452), which spawns “RegSvcs.exe” (process 4360). This is also detected as Lumma.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large is-resized"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-1024x578.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6839" width="650" height="366" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-1024x578.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-768x434.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-1536x868.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-370x209.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63-740x418.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image63.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 650px) 100vw, 650px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Lumma is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>“RegSvcs.exe” (process 4360) is located in “C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework\v4.0.30319\”. This is a part of the Microsoft .NET Framework for version 4.0.30319, and is mostly used for setting up applications that require COM interop. However, “RegSvcs.exe” is known to be abused for registering and executing malicious .NET assemblies by malware. More details can be found in <a href="https://perception-point.io/blog/behind-the-attack-lumma-malware/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">Perception Point’s Lumma Analysis.</a>&nbsp;</p>



<p><br>Process 4360 is seen connecting to its C2, 104.21.88[.]119 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>). HTTP POST requests “<em>/api” </em>to the host <em>ensurerecommendedd[.]pw </em>were observed.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="301" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-1024x301.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6841" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-1024x301.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-300x88.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-768x226.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-1536x452.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-370x109.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-270x79.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1-740x218.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image55-1.png 1902w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">&nbsp;The HTTP POST requests made by Process 4360</figcaption></figure>



<p>In the HTTP POST requests, behavior nearly identical to “T6OBqC4lLuNgq7EqPk6LjxrX.exe” (process 1588) were observed, where various archive files containing Browser (Edge, Firefox) information, <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/4fc7da71-e160-4245-99a3-14db1446c7a1?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">system information, and screenshots</a> were exfiltrated via HTTP. Additionally, an archive file containing Chrome Browser information was observed for Process 4360, and this was opened in a <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/64bf1c5c-d19e-4e8b-9b4a-dbabaa6e6e61?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">this sample</a>.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="433" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-1024x433.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6842" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-1024x433.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-300x127.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-768x325.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-1536x649.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-370x156.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-270x114.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7-740x313.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image7.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>comparison</figcaption></figure>



<p>Unarchiving “file4.zip” reveals various files containing sensitive information related to Chrome. For example, the “Chrome/Default/History&#8221; contained the Chrome Browser history, which included the Google search query “ida pro crack download”. It also included the URL of sites we have previously visited in Analyzing the Behavior in a Sandbox section.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="356" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-1024x356.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6844" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-1024x356.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-300x104.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-768x267.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-1536x534.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-370x129.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-270x94.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83-740x257.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image83.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">A section of “Chrome/Default/History”</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">RedLine</h2>



<p>RedLine is a .NET malware written in C#, and was first identified in 2020. RedLine is known to act as an infostealer that collects information like passwords, credit cards, cookies, location, and more. Additionally, RedLine can be used to deliver more malware, like ransomware, RATs, trojans, miners, and more. More information on RedLine can be found in <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/redline" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s RedLine Malware Trends</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 6280: AppLaunch.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “nNjCpnjCODqx6RJUBNXhaAHF.exe” (process 5764). This spawns “AppLaunch.exe” (process 6280), and is detected as RedLine.</p>



<p></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="582" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-1024x582.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6845" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-1024x582.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-300x170.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-768x436.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-1536x873.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-370x210.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-270x153.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2-740x421.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image2.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">RedLine is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “AppLaunch.exe” (process 6280) is located in “C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework\v4.0.30319\”. This is a part of the Microsoft .NET Framework for version 4.0.30319, and is usually used for launching applications based on the .NET Framework. However, the RedLine payload is known to be injected into “AppLaunch.exe” and other legitimate processes to conduct malicious activities while evading detection. More details can be found in <a href="https://www.netskope.com/jp/blog/redline-stealer-campaign-using-binance-mystery-box-videos-to-spread-github-hosted-payload" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">Netskope’s RedLine Stealer Analysis</a>.&nbsp;</p>



<p><br>Process 6280 was seen repeatedly connecting to 45.15[.]156.187 over port 23929 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1571/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">T1571 &#8211; Non-Standard Port</a>):</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="220" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-1024x220.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6846" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-1024x220.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-300x65.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-768x165.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-1536x331.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-370x80.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-270x58.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78-740x159.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image78.png 1868w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Connections to 45.15[.]156.187 via port 23929 by Process 6280</figcaption></figure>



<p>The contents of the uploaded data were identical, which contained “<em>net.tcp://45.15.156[.]187:23929/</em>”:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="252" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-1024x252.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6848" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-1024x252.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-300x74.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-768x189.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-370x91.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-270x66.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1-740x182.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/12-1.png 1178w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></figure>



<p>The malware configuration for RedLine reveals the C2, Botnet and Keys. The Botnet is “LogsDiller Cloud (Telegram: @logsdillabot)”, according to OSINT this account sells various logs.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="540" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-1024x540.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6849" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-1024x540.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-300x158.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-768x405.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-1536x810.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-370x195.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-270x142.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50-740x390.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image50.png 1562w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Malware Configuration</em> for “AppLaunch.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">RisePro</h2>



<p>RisePro is an information-stealing malware first identified in 2022. It is known to steal credit card, password, and crypto-wallet information. RisePro is written in C++, and employs a system of embedded DLL dependencies. More information on RisePro can be found in <a href="https://any.run/cybersecurity-blog/risepro-malware-communication-analysis/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s RisePro Malware Analysis: Exploring C2 Communication of a New Version</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 3004: 3Pvvg68HWOfBwJ9BdOsWgpEz.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “3Pvvg68HWOfBwJ9BdOsWgpEz.exe” (process 3004), which is detected as RisePro.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="584" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-1024x584.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6850" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-1024x584.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-768x438.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-1536x877.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52-740x422.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image52.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">RisePro is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 3004 runs a command that creates a scheduled task called &#8220;OfficeTrackerNMP131 HR&#8221; and &#8220;OfficeTrackerNMP131 LG&#8221;. This runs &#8220;C:\ProgramData\OfficeTrackerNMP131\OfficeTrackerNMP131.exe&#8221; on an hourly basis and at user logon with the highest privilege, namely &#8220;admin&#8221; (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1497/003/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1497.003 &#8211; Virtualization/Sandbox</a>, and <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1053/005/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">T1053.005 &#8211; Scheduled Task/Job: Scheduled Task</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="277" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-1024x277.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6851" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-1024x277.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-300x81.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-768x208.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-1536x416.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-370x100.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-270x73.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11-740x200.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image11.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 3004 runs the lines surrounded in green.</figcaption></figure>



<p>This executable is dropped by Process 3004:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="83" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1024x83.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6852" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1024x83.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-300x24.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-768x62.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1536x125.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-370x30.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-270x22.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-740x60.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35.png 1650w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 3004 drops “C:\ProgramData\OfficeTrackerNMP131\OfficeTrackerNMP131.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “OfficeTrackerNMP131.exe” (process 3940) is run from the Task Scheduler after the system reboot (T1497.003, and T1053.005). Detonating “OfficeTrackerNMP131.exe” independently inside <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/26c62ba8-5ebd-43bb-8225-fe99eda0ad6f/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">a the sample</a> reveals that it’s a RisePro malware.&nbsp;</p>



<p><br>The “3Pvvg68HWOfBwJ9BdOsWgpEz.exe” (process 3004) creates a file in the startup directory, namely “C:\Users\admin\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup\FANBooster131.lnk”. Persistence may be achieved by adding a program to a startup folder, which causes the referenced program to be executed upon log-in (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1547/001/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1547.001 &#8211; Boot or Logon Autostart Execution: Registry Run Keys / Startup Folder</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="948" height="666" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6853" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69.png 948w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69-300x211.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69-768x540.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69-370x260.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69-270x190.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image69-740x520.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 948px) 100vw, 948px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 3004 creates files in the Startup directory</figcaption></figure>



<p>The “FANBooster131.lnk” is a LNK file, which is a shortcut that points to “C:\Users\admin\AppData\Local\Temp\FANBooster131\FANBooster131.exe”:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="421" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-1024x421.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6854" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-1024x421.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-300x123.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-768x316.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-1536x632.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-370x152.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-270x111.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1-740x304.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/13-1.png 1940w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">“FANBooster131.lnk” points to “FANBooster131.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<p>“FANBooster131.exe” is dropped by Process 3004, and has the exact same hash as “OfficeTrackerNMP131.exe”. The “FANBooster131.exe” (process 7056) starts upon user login. Detonating “FANBooster131.exe” independently inside <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/795434a9-ecea-48c2-a638-4da717d97f03?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">this example </a>reveals that it’s also a RisePro malware.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="83" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-1024x83.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6855" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-1024x83.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-300x24.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-768x62.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-1536x125.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-370x30.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-270x22.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1-740x60.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image35-1.png 1650w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 3004 drops “C:\Users\admin\AppData\Local\Temp\FANBooster131\FANBooster131.exe”</figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 5076: Iq4tpcuftnMe73YjwlKR3YVy.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “Iq4tpcuftnMe73YjwlKR3YVy.exe” (process 5076), which is detected as RisePro.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="577" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-1024x577.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6856" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-1024x577.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-300x169.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-768x433.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-1536x865.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-2048x1154.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-370x208.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-270x152.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/10-2-740x417.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></figure>



<p>Similar to “3Pvvg68HWOfBwJ9BdOsWgpEz.exe” (process 3004), the “Iq4tpcuftnMe73YjwlKR3YVy.exe” (process 5076) creates a scheduled task called &#8220;OfficeTrackerNMP1 LG&#8221; and “OfficeTrackerNMP1 HR“. This runs &#8220;C:\ProgramData\OfficeTrackerNMP1\OfficeTrackerNMP1.exe&#8221; at user logon and on an hourly basis with the highest privilege, namely &#8220;admin&#8221;. Detonating “OfficeTrackerNMP1.exe” independently inside <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/cee52ada-1bf9-470c-91c9-3a631ceb0c2c?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">a sample </a>reveals that it’s also a RisePro malware.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="276" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-1024x276.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6857" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-1024x276.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-300x81.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-768x207.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-1536x414.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-370x100.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-270x73.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41-740x200.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image41.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 5076 runs the lines surrounded in green</figcaption></figure>



<p>The <em>Malware Configuration </em>contained the C2 IP addresses, which was 193[.]223.132.51 and 195[.]20.16.45.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="410" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-1024x410.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6858" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-1024x410.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-300x120.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-768x307.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-1536x614.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-370x148.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-270x108.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1-740x296.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/14-1.png 1850w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Amadey</h2>



<p>Amadey is a very versatile malware first identified in 2018, and can act as a loader or an infostealer. It can perform a wide range of malicious activities, including reconnaissance, data exfiltration, and loading more payloads. More information on Amadey can be found in <a href="https://any.run/malware-trends/amadey" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ANY.RUN’s Amadey Malware Trends</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 4124: 5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns another PrivateLoader “wlC578T8hWfvZ2yJxLzrF38Y.exe” (process 1916). This spawns “5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe.exe” (process 4124), which is detected as Amadey.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="583" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-1024x583.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6859" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-1024x583.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-300x171.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-768x437.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-1536x874.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-370x211.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25-740x421.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image25.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Amadey is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4124 creates a scheduled task called “5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe.exe” that runs <em>&#8220;</em>C:\Users\admin\Pictures\Minor Policy\5RfuRxo3fpxiWkD42DRCixRe.exe<em>&#8220;</em> every minute.</p>



<p></p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="573" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-1024x573.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6861" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-1024x573.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-300x168.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-768x430.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-370x207.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-270x151.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1-740x414.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/15-1.png 1358w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 4124 uses the Task Scheduler to run other applications,</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4124 also changes the autorun value in the registry. The Registry key “HKEY_CURRENT_USER\SOFTWARE\MICROSOFT\WINDOWS\CURRENTVERSION\EXPLORER\USER SHELL FOLDERS” stores the paths to important system folders for the current user, which includes the Desktop, Startup, etc. For “STARTUP”, the value is now “%USERPROFILE%\APPDATA\ROAMING\MICROSOFT\WINDOWS\START MENU\PROGRAMS\STARTUP”. This means that the path to the Startup folder has been changed by Process 4124, and whatever that is in “AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup” will now execute every time upon login (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1547/001/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">T1547.001 &#8211; Boot or Logon Autostart Execution: Registry Run Keys / Startup Folder</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="681" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-1024x681.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6860" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-1024x681.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-300x200.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-768x511.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-370x246.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-270x180.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6-740x492.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image6.png 1058w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 4124 creates autorun value in the registry</figcaption></figure>



<p>These are the files in the “AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup” directory, and include LNK files that point to RisePro malware (<a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/795434a9-ecea-48c2-a638-4da717d97f03/?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">FANBooster131.exe ANY.RUN task</a>, <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/bbf8ea61-bfeb-4fe6-8625-9d2c34e0dcaa?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">PowerExpertNT.exe ANY.RUN task</a>):</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="198" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-1024x198.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6865" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-1024x198.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-300x58.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-768x149.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-1536x297.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-370x72.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-270x52.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65-740x143.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image65.png 1694w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The files under the “AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\Start Menu\Programs\Startup” directory</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Stealc</h2>



<p>Stealc is an information-stealing malware first identified in 2023. It is written in C and utilizes WinAPI functions, and is known to steal sensitive information from browsers and exfiltrate the information to the C2 using HTTP POST requests. The development of Stealc relies on other stealers such as Vidar, Racoon, Redline, and Mars. More information on Stealc can be found in <a href="https://malpedia.caad.fkie.fraunhofer.de/details/win.stealc" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">Malpedia’s Stealc</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 2412: hzQj407t3pAeMkmtH8lxdDg1.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “hzQj407t3pAeMkmtH8lxdDg1.exe” (process 2412), which is detected as Stealc.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="586" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-1024x586.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6866" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-1024x586.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-300x172.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-768x439.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-1536x878.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-370x212.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-270x154.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82-740x423.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image82.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Stealc is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>“hzQj407t3pAeMkmtH8lxdDg1.exe” (process 2412) is located in “C:\Users\admin\Pictures\Minor Policy\”, and is seen connecting to its C2, 5.42.64[.]41 via port 80. HTTP POST request “<em>/40d570f44e84a454.php” </em>to the host 5.42.64[.]41 was observed:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="870" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-1024x870.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6867" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-1024x870.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-300x255.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-768x652.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-370x314.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-270x229.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1-740x629.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/16-1.png 1116w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 2412<em> </em>between 5.42.64[.]41:80 and VM:52705</figcaption></figure>



<p>However, Process 2412 crashes after a while. Detonating “hzQj407t3pAeMkmtH8lxdDg1.exe” independently in <a href="https://app.any.run/tasks/76f11d42-8f58-4f33-a24e-54aa12a10586?utm_source=blog&amp;utm_medium=article&amp;utm_campaign=crackedcantil&amp;utm_content=task&amp;utm_term=300124" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">the task</a> reveals the malware configuration, which includes the C2, Keys, and Strings:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="688" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-1024x688.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6868" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-1024x688.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-300x202.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-768x516.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-1536x1032.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-370x249.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-270x181.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1-740x497.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/17-1.png 1628w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Malware Configuration</em> for “hzQj407t3pAeMkmtH8lxdDg1.exe”</figcaption></figure>

<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Socks5Systemz</h2>



<p>Socks5Systemz is a proxy bot malware that is known to infect devices through PrivateLoader and Amadey. Socks5Systemz turns infected devices into traffic-forwarding proxies for malicious traffic and connects to its C2 server with a DGA. More information on Socks5Systemz can be found in <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/socks5systemz-proxy-service-infects-10-000-systems-worldwide/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">BleepingComputer’s Socks5Systemz proxy service infects 10,000 systems worldwide</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 6364: DTPanelQT.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “69wM7sx_H1qc_If9hwYqEhWr.exe” (process 4960), which spawns “69wM7sx_H1qc_If9hwYqEhWr.tmp” (process 5560). This spawns “DTPanelQT.exe” (process 6364), which is detected as Socks5Systemz.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="581" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-1024x581.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6870" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-1024x581.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-300x170.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-768x436.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-1536x872.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-370x210.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-270x153.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27-740x420.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image27.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Socks5Systemz is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 6364 was seen connecting to its C2, 185.196.8[.]22 via port 80 (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1071/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1071 &#8211; Application Layer Protocol</a>). Numerous GET requests to the host <em>ercwwol[.]ua</em> were observed:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="390" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-1024x390.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6871" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-1024x390.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-300x114.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-768x293.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-1536x586.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-370x141.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-270x103.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84-740x282.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image84.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">&nbsp;The HTTP GET requests and the <em>Network Stream </em>for Process 6364</figcaption></figure>



<p>It is also seen connecting to 176.9.47[.]240 via port 2023, which is a non-typical protocol and port pairing (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1571/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1571 &#8211; Non-Standard Port</a>):</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="249" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-1024x249.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6872" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-1024x249.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-300x73.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-768x187.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-1536x374.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-370x90.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-270x66.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1-740x180.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/18-1.png 1982w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Connections to 176.9.47[.]240 via port 2023 by Process 6364</figcaption></figure>



<p>The data sent to 176.9.47[.]240 via port 2023 appears to be a bunch of IP addresses and the port in the syntax “<em>[IP ADDRESS]:[PORT];</em>”, and all the contents were identical:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="292" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-1024x292.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6873" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-1024x292.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-300x86.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-768x219.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-1536x439.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-370x106.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-270x77.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47-740x211.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image47.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em></figcaption></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 4120: TacDecoLIB.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup.exe” (process 4440) spawns “H0jrwuNM7IG2q266V2EfAiVo.exe” (process 4548), which spawns “rjcJoThBdrYU.exe” (process 6880), which also spawns “rjcJoThBdrYU.tmp” (process 4900). This spawns “TacDecoLIB.exe” (process 4120), which is also detected as Socks5Systemz.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="523" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-1024x523.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6874" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-1024x523.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-300x153.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-768x392.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-1536x785.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-370x189.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-270x138.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-585x300.png 585w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39-740x378.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image39.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Socks5Systemz process tree</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 4120 was also seen connecting to its C2, 185.196.8[.]22 via port 80, and 176.9.47[.]240 via port 2023. This is the same as “DTPanelQT.exe” (process 6364), except to the host <em>aitmrzn[.]ru </em>instead of <em>ercwwol[.]ua. </em>The data sent to 176.9.47[.]240 via port 2023 appears to be a bunch of IP addresses and the port, which was identical to Process 6364.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="77" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-1024x77.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6875" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-1024x77.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-300x23.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-768x58.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-1536x116.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-370x28.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-270x20.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17-740x56.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image17.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">&nbsp;The HTTP GET requests by Process 4120</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">STOP</h2>



<p>STOP is ransomware that encrypts user data, and the encrypted file extensions include .hhaz, .djvuu, .ljaz, and more. DJVU is a variant of the STOP ransomware and can include several layers of obfuscation which makes analysis more difficult. STOP/DJVU was first seen in 2018, and known to use AES-256, and Salsa20 for encryption. DJVU is known to collaborate with other malware, for example, it works with infostealer malware to steal sensitive information before the files are encrypted. More information on STOP/DJVU can be found in <a href="https://blogs.blackberry.com/en/2022/09/djvu-the-ransomware-that-seems-strangely-familiar" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">BlackBerry’s DJVU: The Ransomware That Seems Strangely Familiar</a>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 6412: TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc.exe</h3>



<p>The initial PrivateLoader “setup[.]exe” (process 4440) spawns “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc[.]exe” (process 4944), which spawns “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc[.]exe” (process 6380), which spawns “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc[.]exe” (process 6808). This finally spawns “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc[.]exe” (process 6412). This is detected as STOP.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="580" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-1024x580.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6876" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-1024x580.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-300x170.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-768x435.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-1536x871.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-370x210.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-270x153.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20-740x419.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image20.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">STOP is detected</figcaption></figure>



<p>It uses the line “<em>&#8211;Admin IsNotAutoStart IsNotTask”, </em>meaning that it runs using admin privileges, and specifies to not automatically start or run as a task. The purpose is likely to allow the infostealers (Lumma, RedLine, RisePro, Amadey, Stealc) to steal sensitive information before the ransomware encrypts the files.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="241" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-1024x241.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6877" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-1024x241.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-300x71.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-768x181.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-1536x362.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-2048x483.png 2048w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-370x87.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-270x64.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/19-740x174.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The <em>Network Stream </em></figcaption></figure>



<p>It was seen making HTTP GET requests “<em>/test2/get.php?pid=47DCC01E8C1FE7754757A5DC66C0F42F&amp;first=true</em>” to the host <em>zexeq[.]com</em>, and the response contained a public key (in green). The MAC address for the system is 52:54:00:4a:ad:11, and converting this to Upper Case and generating the MD5 hash reveals that it is identical to the string in the GET request (in red):</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="367" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-1024x367.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6878" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-1024x367.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-300x108.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-768x275.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-1536x551.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-370x133.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-270x97.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75-740x265.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image75.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Process 6328: TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc.exe</h3>



<p>After the system reboot, the process “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc.exe” (process 2404) spawns “TzjwSXczmD2hOVANbz7L7Roc.exe” (process 6328), which is detected as STOP.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="580" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-1024x580.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6879" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-1024x580.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-300x170.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-768x435.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-1536x871.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-370x210.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-270x153.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90-740x419.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/image90.png 1999w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">STOP process tree</figcaption></figure>



<p>This time, it uses the line “<em>&#8211;AutoStart</em>” to automatically start:</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="156" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-1024x156.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6880" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-1024x156.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-300x46.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-768x117.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-1536x234.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-370x56.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-270x41.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1-740x113.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/20-1.png 1774w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The lines surrounded in green are used to specify the task options</figcaption></figure>



<p>Process 6328 creates a file “geo[1].json” under “C:\Users\admin\AppData\Local\Microsoft\Windows\INetCache\IE\J0KBFYBW\”. This JSON file contains EXIF, which includes location information such as the City, Country, Ip, Latitude, Longitude, Region, etc.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="994" height="808" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6881" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1.png 994w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1-300x244.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1-768x624.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1-370x301.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1-270x219.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/21-1-740x602.png 740w" sizes="(max-width: 994px) 100vw, 994px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The attributes and contents of “geo[1].json” in <em>Static Discovering</em></figcaption></figure>



<p>A few seconds after reboot and login, it starts encrypting files and appends the “.hhaz” extension (<a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1486/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener nofollow">T1486 &#8211; Data Encrypted for Impact</a>).</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="157" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-1024x157.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6882" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-1024x157.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-300x46.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-768x117.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-1536x235.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-370x57.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-270x41.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1-740x113.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/22-1.png 1608w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Process 6328 encrypts various files</figcaption></figure>



<p>The files with the .hhaz extension contained the string “<em>{36A698B9-D67C-4E07-BE82-0EC5B14B4DF5}</em>” at the very end. This is a mutex, and is used by ransomware to avoid double-encrypting files.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img decoding="async" loading="lazy" width="1024" height="412" src="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-1024x412.png" alt="CrackedCantil malware analysis" class="wp-image-6883" srcset="/cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-1024x412.png 1024w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-300x121.png 300w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-768x309.png 768w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-1536x618.png 1536w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-370x149.png 370w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-270x109.png 270w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1-740x298.png 740w, /cybersecurity-blog/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/23-1.png 1900w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">The contents of a .hhaz file, including the mutex</figcaption></figure>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Conclusion</h2>



<p>This deep dive explored the dangers of cracked software, traits and behaviors of several notorious malware families, and how they can work together to deliver a powerful infection in a symphonious manner.&nbsp;</p>



<p>The malware symphony in this CrackedCantil included Loaders, Infostealers, Cryptominers, Proxy Bot malware, and Ransomware. The Loaders (PrivateLoader, Smoke) dropped several notorious malware onto the system, the Infostealers (Lumma, RedLine, RisePro, Amadey, Stealc) stole various sensitive information before the ransomware encrypted the files, the Proxy Bot malware (Socks5Systemz) turned the system into a proxy bot, and the Ransomware (STOP) encrypted the files and demanded ransom for recovery.&nbsp;</p>



<p>This malware was named “CrackedCantil” by the author (of the article, not the malware), Lena (aka LambdaMamba).</p>

                                            data-cell-id="C17"
                    data-col-index="2"
                    data-row-index="16"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        Communicate using OSI application layer protocols to avoid detection.                    </td>
                                        </tr>
                            <tr class="wpdt-cell-row " >
                                            <td class="wpdt-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="B18"
                    data-col-index="1"
                    data-row-index="17"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        T1571: Non-Standard Port                    </td>
                                                <td class="wpdt-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="C18"
                    data-col-index="2"
                    data-row-index="17"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        Communicate using a protocol and port pairing that are typically not associated.                    </td>
                                        </tr>
                            <tr class="wpdt-cell-row " >
                                <td class="wpdt-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="A19"
                    data-col-index="0"
                    data-row-index="18"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        TA0040: Impact                    </td>
                                                <td class="wpdt-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="B19"
                    data-col-index="1"
                    data-row-index="18"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        T1486: Data Encrypted for Impact                    </td>
                                                <td class="wpdt-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="C19"
                    data-col-index="2"
                    data-row-index="18"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                        Encrypt data on target systems or on large numbers of systems in a network to interrupt availability to system and network resources.                    </td>
                                        </tr>
                            <tr class="wpdt-cell-row " >
                                <td class="wpdt-cell  wpdt-empty-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="A20"
                    data-col-index="0"
                    data-row-index="19"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                                            </td>
                                                <td class="wpdt-cell  wpdt-empty-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="B20"
                    data-col-index="1"
                    data-row-index="19"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                                            </td>
                                                <td class="wpdt-cell  wpdt-empty-cell "
                                            data-cell-id="C20"
                    data-col-index="2"
                    data-row-index="19"
                    style="                    padding:10px;
                    "
                    >
                                                            </td>
                                        </tr>
                    </table>

</style>




<p>*Not every tactics and techniques involved are included due to the complexity</p>
